0525953728|2.0|There are plenty of in depth reviews of this book already, so I'll be brief with this one.  Basically, the book is just another book to add to the loads of other mediocre works on military operations in Iraq and Afghanistan with nothing to set it apart.  The writing is not impressive, there is no suspense or anything particularly interesting, and the only thing kept me going was the promise of \\"setting the record\\" straight.  Most of the chapters are just broad overviews of operations filled with just enough detail to let the reader know how awesome the author is.  There is plenty of chest thumping and practical joking throughout the pages if that's your thing though.<br /><br />One thing that bothers me is thinking about what this book could have been.  When I think of books about secret operations that are not sanctioned by the authors' communities, \\"Kill Bin Laden\\" by Dalton Fury is one of the first that comes to mind and I can't help but compare \\"No Easy Day\\" to it.  While there are similarities in the books, there are quite a few differences between that really distinguish them from each other.  Dalton Fury made his book an ode to the men he fought with, and never tried to inflate his own ego which Mark Owen seems to do the entire book.  Dalton Fury really does set the record straight, and does so without badmouthing his superiors or implying they are less than capable.  Finally, \\"Kill Bin Laden\\" was written years after the events it describes, and was not published during the media frenzy that followed.  \\"No Easy Day\\" really just appears to be a book published to capitalize on the fleeting interest of a recent even and make a quick buck.<br /><br />Most of the cons to this book are brought up frequently in the 1 start reviews.  I agree with most of them but I didn't find the cons egregious enough to warrant a 1 start review, however.  Certainly not the best book I have ever read or even a good book, but not the worst either.  There is nothing earth shattering and I don't really see how Mark Owen sets the record straight.  He confirms what most people already know: Seal Team 6 did a helo raid on Bin Laden's compound, a helo went down, and they shot Bin Laden.  There are a few more details, but that's about the extent of it.  Save your money if you are looking for anything with much substance.

B009G7ZYUO|1.0|I was a fan since first hearing an advance copy of the cassette back in 92.  I still remember playing the album for my friends and hearing them say \\"this sounds ok, but it will never catch on\\".  For once I was right!!  Anyway, Sony and the band should be ashamed of this release, really over $70?  Ok let me break it down for everyone.<br /><br />1 - The self titled debut which has already been released numerous times.<br />2 - Three b-sides which most fans that initially jumped on the band wagon already have because of the fan base's thirst for more music by the band.<br />3 - Demo tracks that were always available from day one.  It is well publicized that they sold these at their early shows, and the demos were always around on the tape trading market, and in recent years on youtube.<br />4 - A VHS which was later released with some pretty cool show footage, and videos that also formed part of the same VHS, or can be viewed on the web. This DVD is available here on Amazon for roughly $12, I've seen it for half that at other stores.<br />5 - A concert from a few years back that is on youtube.<br />6 - The album on vinyl<br />7 - A book with unreleased images and linear notes by Chuck D.<br /><br />If you are a die hard fan, you already probably have all of this material, and perhaps want the vinyl, or are a collectionist and would like to have the boxed set it self.  That's cool, I understand that, but for the average fan?  This definitely is not for them.  For someone like myself, I have purchased the album multiple times, including as part of the boxed set that was released a few years back, isn't it awfully greedy that you release this one more time? 1615470158|5.0|One of the big challenges to being an early adopter of any software product is the lack of documentation that accompanies it.  This isn't a big deal if the software is intuitive, and error messages are well crafted to help you get through them.  Unfortunately, while PowerPivot is amazingly powerful, and while you can do some amazing things with it, DAX (the formula language within PowerPivot) is neither intuitive nor are the error messages helpful to an Excel pro.  As the owner of [...] and a Microsoft MVP, I'd like to think that I know a little about working with Excel, but despite the fact that I've been a huge advocate for PowerPivot, and using it extensively for 2 years, DAX has remained a power that has been just out of reach.    That ended this past week.    Last week I bought a bought Rob Collie's new book \\"DAX Formulas for PowerPivot: The Excel Pro's Guide to Mastering DAX\\". When you are ready to take the DAX journey, you MUST have this book.    Rob's writing style is identical to that you'll find on his blog at [...]. In fact, the only difference is that he doesn't fill up any real estate with movie quotes.  What you do get is a huge amount of insightful information.    Four days of working through this book with my own data and I am now making some amazing stuff that eluded me before.    What I love about this book is that Rob sat down and carefully thought through how an Excel pro should learn DAX, then started at the beginning. It progresses logically, is very easy to read, and actually gets you there. Yes, you need to practice, but weren't you going to do that anyway? And when you practice...    There's something really important between these pages that I doubt you'll ever see in the advertising. With any programming language (yes, Excel formulas fit that bill, as do DAX formulas) there are two critical things to learn. The first is Syntax (how you do something), and the second is how to debug something. Rob gives you both, which is critical on the path to mastery. He has some nice little charts that explain how filters are applied to the CALCULATE function, (which is like SUMIF on steroids.) With those charts, you can actually follow the steps to figure out exactly why your measure is not doing what you want. (Trust me, it was doing this that actually helped me finally get that Eureka moment that I've been looking for!)    Four days. I've gone from not being able to make anything but the most simple DAX measure to the point where I can create some pretty cool CALCULATE functions (my new best friend), with nested date/time intelligent functions as well. I've been pursuing that for TWO YEARS!    After struggling with DAX for so long, I think it's fair to say that I wouldn't have been able to break the DAX barrier without this book.    If you're struggling with DAX, or just getting ready to start the PowerPivot journey get his book. You owe it to yourself.

0375507256|3.0|Have you ever flown a small plane through a cloud bank?  You're cruising along in the clear blue and there in front of you sits a big, puffy cumulus cloud.  It's kind of thrilling the first time.  You approach this thing that looks so solid.  Your heart beats a little faster, anticipating the impact, and before you know it you're right up against it and *** poof ***.  Nothing.  Just silence and claustrophobic, wispy whiteness outside the glass.  In another minute, you're through.  If you imagine real hard, you might feel a little damp on the other side, but not much else.  But only if you imagine real, real hard.<br /><br />That's kind of like the feeling I experienced while reading \\"Cloud Atlas\\".  I thought, 'well, look at this', and plunged in.  And now I'm out the other side.  No impact.  Just thin air and water vapor.<br /><br />For the clever conceit - a sextet, for the fluid prose, for its droll humor and a few laugh-out-loud jokes, for its enjoyable sci-fi rompiness, for its bite-size chunkiness that allows one to pick it up and put it down and not miss much, for some bits of wonderful, memorable word play, for all that \\"Cloud Atlas\\" is an easy book to recommend.<br /><br />But for its dime-store environmentalism, for its liberal use of dated sci-fi tropes, for its graphomania, for the pretense of 'linked' stories, for the ineffectual swipe at the post-modern, for the (to my ear) at times horrible, clunky dialects, for the headache I got trying to decipher the 'bridge' piece (Sloosha's Crossin'), I'm hesitant to recommend the book and, admittedly, somewhat baffled by all the praise it has received.  It won't be a re-read.<br /><br />David Mitchell's talent is enviable, his intelligence remarkable, his work ethic evident.  But after reading reviews claiming that \\"Cloud Atlas\\" evokes \\"the eloquence of Nabokov\\" and \\"the depth of Murakami\\" I begin to wonder whether those reviewers have ever read any Nabokov, and who was it who told them that Murakami was \\"deep\\".<br /><br />In \\"Slow Learner\\" Thomas Pynchon warns writers of the danger of trying to develop plot and characters around an abstraction like \\"a thermodynamic coinage or the data in a guidebook\\", as it usually results in the kind of apprentice writing exercise that \\"Cloud Atlas\\" too often resembles.  I'm not being mean here, but had I read this four or five years ago, I probably would have concluded that someday, somebody is gonna be making a film of this.  Starring Tom Hanks.<br /><br />I, like most readers, am aware of and sensitive to the environmental and social concerns that Mitchell (more than) touches on in the book, but his proselytizing tone in introducing these important themes is counterproductive, heavy-handed.  If he would insist that it wasn't his goal to underscore the evils of racism, rapacious colonialism, environmental pollution, militarism, et al., then one may rightly wonder what the point of the book is supposed to be.  If it's \\"just a story\\" then it fails as one story, or even a sextet of stories.  As written, the characters just aren't interesting enough.  As a novel, the structure is gimmicky, its logical sequencing tenuous.  As a collection of short stories, it's just weak.<br /><br />Certainly there are moments of luminosity - Sonmi's isolation, Frobisher's picayune complaints - but these are mere particles of light.  Taken separately, the episodes are not sturdy enough to stand on their own (with the possible exception of \\"Letters from Zedelghem\\", though it would require a great deal of 'filling in').  It almost felt as if Mitchell couldn't decide between locking the thing down in an iron-clad plot or allowing it to float free like a none-too-arbitrary collection of ruminations on a theme.<br /><br />Whichever he chose - and I'm still not clear which - it didn't work for me.<br /><br />Despite its size (approaching 600 pages) \\"Cloud Atlas\\" is peopled with characters woefully underwritten, spouting a watery, politically correct, laughably anachronistic kerygma and ending up nowhere.  Apparently.  An exquisitely detailed, utterly directionless, scrupulously elegant chart of floating water vapor, or by any other name a Cloud Atlas.chronistic kerygma and ending up nowhere.  Apparently.  An exquisitely detailed, utterly directionless, scrupulously elegant chart of floating water vapor, or by any other name a Cloud Atlas.

B00006F7S4|5.0|...Those are just a few words I use when asked to describe Sea Change, one of my favorite albums of all-time. I'm a huge Beck fan and I admire the way he's able to evolve and pursue a radically different sound on every album. His constant reinvention and knack for hopping genres is a rare gift that few musicians have been able to pull off so successfully. As much as I enjoy his more bizarre and experimental work, I often find that many of my favorite Beck compositions feature stripped down arrangements and more pensive lyrics. He's done some amazing work, yet Sea Change still stands the tallest in my book.<br /><br />You don't have to be nursing a wounded heart or be at rock bottom to connect with this album. Anyone can enjoy the melancholy tunes and sad tales that he weaves here, yet it works best when you're hurting. No matter what may be troubling you, this album with help soothe it. The story here is certainly tragic, as songs like \\"Guess I'm Doing Fine\\" and \\"Lost Cause\\" quickly reiterate. However, it would be a mistake to assume that this album is just another whiny, self-indulgent breakup album. Beck paints with an entirely finer canvas here, weaving intricate tales of loss and pain with hope and perseverance. The album starts off with darkness, picking up the pieces of a fractured relationship and eventually ends with the advice, \\"Let it pass on the side of the road\\". It's haunting and wonderfully done.<br /><br />The production is nothing short of perfect. Beck's voice effortlessly carries every song, allowing the players around him to add their subtle instrumental touches into each track. The production itself is warm and creates an intimate atmosphere that's easy on the ears. It's one of the best sounding records I've ever heard and Nigel Godrich never fails to bring out the best sounds and performances with any artist he works with.<br /><br />Sea Change is life-affirming and gorgeous. It may throw some fans for a loop, especially if you're expecting the fun and energetic genre mashups of his other work. Don't be turned off: this is Beck at his most real and direct. The result is a modern masterpiece. It may not make you feel better, but it will remind you that someone else understands what you're going through. B00AEBBAEK|5.0|Murder. Cannibalism. Death. Obsession. Revenge. Blood. Goth makeup. And lots of razors -- \\"at last, my arm is complete again!\\" Sweeney Todd exults.<br /><br />Somehow it doesn't come as a shock to me that Tim Burton adapted Stephen Sondheim's musical \\"Sweeney Todd - The Demon Barber of Fleet Street\\" -- or that he somehow spun it into something so delicious. That dark, grotesque, hilariously melodramatic story is perfectly suited to Burton's style, and Johnny Depp is absolutely stunning as the titular bloody barber.<br /><br />The malignant Judge Turpin (Alan Rickman) lusts after the wife of Benjamin Barker (Depp), so he convicts Barker of a crime he didn't commit, and enfolds his family into his evil hands.<br /><br />But fifteen years later, the Barker returns to London and sets up a barber shop over Mrs. Lovett's ghastly meat pie store. Of course, he's enraged when he learns that his wife was raped and since poisoned herself, and that his daughter is the ward of the lecherous Judge. Enraged and maddened, Barker renames himself \\"Sweeney Todd\\" and vows revenge.<br /><br />And he finds that he LOVES using his razors for a far bloodier task than shaving. With the help of Mrs. Lovett (Helena Bonham Carter) -- who finds a thrifty use for those bodies -- Todd cuts a bloody swathe through all who have wronged him. And when his daughter is punished for refusing to marry the cruel Judge, Sweeney closes in to get his revenge at last.<br /><br />There's always been a gothic look to Burton's movies, and he's always dabbled in very twisted, macabre storylines. And he really tops himself with \\"Sweeney Todd - The Demon Barber of Fleet Street\\" -- London is painted in black, white and grey, right down to the ghoulish faces of the characters, and their bleak little dens of horror. And songs -- lots of magnificently horrible songs.<br /><br />But Burton pretty obviously adores the combination of gory grotesquerie and very, very sick humour (\\"They don't commit sins of the flesh, so it's pretty fresh\\"). And he doesn't try to make Sweeney or Mrs. Lovett palatable, thankfully. While we sympathize with Sweeney's losses, and the horrors that have changed him into the Demon Barber, you just can't pass over scenes where they sing, \\"It's man devouring man, my dear!\\" \\"Then who are we to deny it in here?\\"<br /><br />There are some moments that relieve this gory gothic parade -- there's a sweet love story between Sweeney's daughter and a young sailor. And the plot becomes progressively darker toward the end (yes, it CAN get worse), when the plot throws us some shocking new twists, resulting in a Grecian-tragedy finale soaked in even more gore.<br /><br />Oh yes, there's blood. Tons of it. It spurts like Monty Python's bloodier sketches, which ends up being more hilarious than yucky -- as is the casual introduction of cannibal meat pies. And there are some spectacularly gross moments, like a finger found in one of the pies.<br /><br />Burton uses some of his favorite actors in this one, particularly Depp and Bonham-Carter. Depp is THE perfect ideal Sweeney Todd -- his creepy eyes, pallid face and still, almost seductive manner are perfect for the maddened murderous barber. He goes through the movie slashing his razors at the world, and injects a real creepiness into scenes like Sweeney cooing at his \\"friends.\\"<br /><br />While she's only a passable singer, Bonham-Carter is eerily wholehearted as Todd's equally amoral partner-in-crime, who is quite happy to assist him.... and make tastier pies in the process. Rickman is wonderfully loathsome as the Judge, and Sacha Baron Cohen has a small but priceless role as Pirello, a huckster acquaintance of Todd's who starts causing trouble. He really steals his scenes.<br /><br />Most directors would have prettified, sanitized and defanged the grotesque \\"Sweeney Todd - The Demon Barber of Fleet Street,\\" but Tim Burton and Johnny Depp revel in the gore and madness. 1615470158|5.0|One of the characteristics of a really good, classic movie is that it has a lot of memorable dialog.  I could go on for hours quoting one-liners from The Blues Brothers or Princess Bride.  Likewise, I think a good book leaves the reader with gems to ponder and to stimulate ideas.  Such has been my recent experience reading Rob Collie's \\"DAX Formulas for PowerPivot, The Excel Pro's Guide to Mastering DAX\\".  The title doesn't have the allure of an action thriller but it's been a while since I've read a book so entertaining and effective at teaching a valuable skill.<br /><br />I've challenged myself to learn more advanced DAX while honing my skills with PowerPivot and SSAS Tabular Model design.  I understand a lot of the basic concepts but I really need to get deep into practical business calculations.  But unlike the past few years of my BI career that I've spent using IT development and performance tuning tools, I am finding my self embracing the PowerPivot designer in Excel.  In recent BI client work, I see a growing need to help business professionals do their own analysis and self-service reporting.  To educate them, I need to think more like those business users.<br /><br />I have an arsenal of books and resources that are all really good.  However, I chose to focus my attention on using DAX in the Excel add-in for now and then I'll come back to the topic of solution architecture and larger-scale design a little later on.  For now, I'm reading Rob Collie's book page by page and working through the examples.  The pages just ooze with personality and have no shortage of opinion and advice.  I like this quote:<br /><br />\\"Much like the heat and pressure in the earth's crust seize the occasional pocket of carbon and transform it into a diamond, the demands of the modern world `recruit' a certain kind of person and forge them into an Excel Pro.\\"<br /><br />...that's profound.  This isn't a book written for programmers or IT professionals but for the new generation of Excel business super-users.  Rob's book is unlike most of those technical books that decompose a software tool and lay each part on the examining room table, detailing its features and nuances.  He tells you what he thinks and how he has used the tool successfully and then moves on without explaining all of the ways to \\"do it the other way\\".  The pages are full of screen shots, images and diagrams with call-outs and commentary.  Reading it makes me feel like he's personally showing me how to access all the cool trap doors and secrets.  As a technical book writer, it's very apparent to me that this book - although I'm sure it's been thoroughly proof-read and reviewed, has not been subjected to the rigid formatting rules and unforgiving styling of a large publisher.  In places, it's almost like a hand-written manuscript with the author's doodles & personal notes in the margins.<br /><br />There are a lot of good books on the market about PowerPivot, tabular models in SSAS and DAX.  This book focuses entirely on DAX for the Excel professional and teaches DAX calculations and expressions, and not queries.  For me, this is the best book I've found to learn how to think like my business clients and to understand the practical application for the self-service BI designer.<br /><br />More information about this book is available here on the PowerPivot(pro) blog: [...]b's book is unlike most of those technical books that decompose a software tool and lay each part on the examining room table, detailing its features and nuances. She has really tried so hard to embrace this hard image she adopted after she was assaulted and I feel like she was rushed into becoming something that doesn't always fit her aesthetic well.  I've had this issue with her since `Rated R' when she first incorporated this level of edginess into her music.  When Rihanna first popped on the scene she was yet another pop princess with little to differentiate her from every other young singer in the game other than a slight accent in her singing.  Then she dropped `Good Girl Gone Bad' and left us with `Umbrella' and `Disturbia', two tracks that cemented her in the game in a big way.<br /><br />She had the right amount of edge to sustain her career.<br /><br />Sadly after her life altering altercation with Chris Brown she became intent on lacing her every move with a hardness that almost at times embraces the abuse and uses it as a catapult into a newfound musical territory for her.  It works at times, but I have been turned off by Rihanna's intent on throwing out four letter works and singing about killing people as a way to convince us that she is hardcore.  It feels forced and uncomfortable.<br /><br />When she fits so comfortable into tracks like `Diamonds' and `We Found Love' why is there a need to try so hard.<br /><br />`Phresh Out the Runway' is the perfect example of her just sounding stupid.  Yes, let's color every sentence with twelve F-words and try, yet again, to be all gangster when it never sounds right coming out of my mouth.  It doesn't really stop there though.  The poor choices on this album are pretty detrimental.  `Numb' is a vocal fumble, leaving Rihanna sounding really bad.  Her attempt to give an edge to her verse deliver just makes her sound out of tune.  The trend continues right into `Pour It Up', which is atrocious.  The lyrical content is obsurd (singing about wasting money in a strip club does not make you sound as cool as you think it does), but the vocals are the worst she has ever contributed to any track.  She comes across like a young teenager trying to look cool in front of her peers.  \\"Look at me, I have lots of money and like naked women!\\"  She even uses the line \\"I'm going dumb with all my friends\\" and that is exactly what it sounds like.  The disco throwback that is `Right Now' was ill advised.  It doesn't fit and loses all edge and sounds like a dated misstep regardless of the forced techno vibe.  `Get Over It' is also kind of bad.  It just feels poorly executed and boring.  I hate the lazy development of the chorus.<br /><br />But nothing on this album is as offensive or as tacky as `Nobody's Business'.  I really think that someone should have sat Rihanna down and informed her that the tragedy that took place between her and Chris Brown became the world's business when it was plastered all over the place, and while she has made a career out of exploiting that situation and has effective completely remodeled her image, singing a song WITH Brown basically sticking it to everyone who has supported her through this is just in poor taste.  It doesn't help that the song is dated and dumb, but I'm just insulted and offended that she thought this was a good idea.  If you have forgiven him and love him and want to be with him then yay for you, but singing such a tacky song with him about your affections was just wrong.<br /><br />So that's a lot of bad; but when she hits high marks they are really high.<br /><br />`Diamonds' is intoxicating, alluring and the best single Rihanna has dropped in four albums.  `Loveeeeee Song' has such soulful flare and delicate vocal delivery that proves Rihanna has the vocal chops.  `What Now' is astonishingly good, with such vocal highs for Rihanna that I'm shocked and amazed it's on the same album as `Pour It Up'. The dual `Love Without Tragedy/Mother Mary' opens with a beat obviously inspired by `Message in a Bottle' and it works really well.  It does feel a tad disjointed in the segues (it almost feels like three separate songs) but it isn't disjointed enough for me to find the song offputting, especially since Rihanna flows into each section flawlessly.  `Get Over it' may be my favorite island inspired Rihanna song.  I find her attempt to bring island flare forced a lot of the time, but she gets this one very close to right.  `Lost in Paradise' closes the album on a nice note.  It isn't her finest moment, but it has a real uplift to it that makes it feel almost rock ballad epic in parts.<br /><br />So, in closing, I'm disappointed in where Rihanna has taken her career.  Reinventing her career and going the hard/edgy route was not a bad choice, but she hasn't learnt how to balance that out yet.  `Loud' was the closest she came, but she got too tacky with `Talk That Talk' and seems to have lost her way entirely here.  Despite having four songs I actually LOVE, this album as a whole is a mess.br /><br />Then there are some admirable tries that don't nail it but come close.  `Jump' features a ripped chorus from the 90's classic `Pony' and it also features a SICK beat that makes this must have club music. Still, I love the song.  My favorite track is probably `Stop Standing There'.  It balances the ballad aspects with the pop overtones with a fresh effect.<br /><br />Then the album falls off a bit.<br /><br />`Smile' is flirty but conceptually dumb and lazy.  It became apparent at this track that this album was going to blur into itself after a while.  There really is no definition in these songs.  They are just marginally differing variations of the track that came before it (or two songs before it).  Still, the chorus is catchy (aren't they all?) but that verse structure is unfortunate.  `I Love You' is just stupid.  This is the definition of lazy.  The lyrics are ridiculous, that chorus, so skeletal and pre-teenish.  `Everybody Hurts' carries the torch into yet another uninspired `boo-hoo my life is so hard' song that has no relevance because it doesn't say anything at all.  It also suffers from sounding like an early 90's track that was rejected off a Four Non Blondes album or something.<br /><br />A little redemption is found in `Not Enough' that has some dramatics in the right places to give it something special.<br /><br />It's short lived, since the next song is `4 Real', which is just bland for bland's sake.  It goes nowhere and does nothing with nothing.  The hate that `I'm for real' line, which is so dated.  `Darlin' is stripped down, which should have been a nice change of pace, but it comes across boring.  There just isn't anything special about half of this album.  Like I said, they sound like the song that came before it.  No effort was made to make these songs unique or worth listening to even.<br /><br />I really love `Remember When', which is emotional and heartfelt and the chorus is spectacularly constructed.  Like I said, I skip to track 12 (with a brief stop at track 9).<br /><br />`Goodbye' tries something a little different actually, giving it a fairytale like quality and it somewhat works.  Not entirely (her vocals come off strained in parts) but the intention was right and I respect her finally trying something different (it doesn't sound like anything else on the album).  It was a nice way to end the album, even if I only find it marginally effective.  The album doesn't really end there though; for some versions have the extended cut of `Alice' from Tim Burton's miserable `Alice in Wonderland'.  I've always defended Avril, for I feel like she has a great voice, and she demonstrates that here.  Sadly, the song is a forced piece of screaming that never ends.  So, while she shows off her vocal range, she doesn't show any restraint and by the end I wanted to shove a sock in her mouth.<br /><br />TOO MUCH!<br /><br />So, sadly, this album is a mess.  It just doesn't work.  There was a time when I really loved Avril, but it is apparent that she is losing touch with what makes you a relevant artist; change.  If you want to be considered a true artist you have to find that balance of `being yourself' and `adapting to the change in the times'.  Avril is still stuck in late 90's/early 00's view of alternative punk and she hasn't figured out how tomake her sound more modern.<br /><br />Better luck next time.make her sound more modern.<br /><br />Better luck next time.

B000026OW3|3.0|Of course Rubenstein is a very good pianist and arguable the best interpreter of Chopin. However, I would argue against that point at times. Sometimes Rubenstein plays with too much rubato.<br /><br />Chopin's own playing was very steady-paced with the accompaniment hand and only performed the rubato with his other hand when he did. He stressed evenness a necessity.<br /><br />And the Sonatas as performed by Rubenstein leaves something to be desired. In Sonata #2 in B Flat minor, Mvt. 1, it begans Grave, then Doppio Movimento. For the Doppio Moviment, Chopin uses pedal markings and legato (a definite mark of Chopin). Rubenstein plays without the pedal or legato. To me, without the pedal and the legato, the piece loses much of its breath-taking beauty. I absolutely LOVE that Sonata...just not how Rubenstein plays it.<br /><br />Also, the etudes (perhaps what many people know Chopin best for, e.g. &#34;Revolutionary&#34; Etude) are not included in this collection.<br /><br />And though the preludes are include, they are of such a poor quality that all they do is waste space. These are obviously very old recordings, though I mean that in the worst possible way.<br /><br />All in all, I do not hate Rubenstein. But I would HIGH RECOMMEND Chopin Complete Edition by Various Artists (sold here on Amazon) over this.

B0002IQNAG|4.0|This sequel to Night of the Living Dead by George A. Romero has probably solidified him as the father of the modern zombie movie. If all western philosophy is footnotes to Plato, then all modern zombie movies are footnotes to Romero's first three zombie films (Day of the Dead is the third). They've all been remade and countless spin-offs and imitations are out there.<br /><br />The movie follows a few survivors in the midst of the zombie apocalypse (though they don't call it that). The action begins in Philadelphia, where a television station is trying to stay on air as long as it can to give people the information they need (and max out their ratings). Even if the information they have is becoming hopelessly out of date. The air-traffic reporter takes his girlfriend (one of the producers at the station) in his helicopter to find safety somewhere else, like maybe Canada.<br /><br />Meanwhile, the military is trying to round up people and get them to safe locations. This effort is not going well since some people are resisting. The racist members of the military use the situation as an excuse to open up on minorities. This part of the film is probably the most jarring and seems like social commentary thrown in for its own sake rather than for narrative coherence. Eventually a couple of soldiers join up with the TV station staff in the helicopter and head out looking for safety.<br /><br />As they fly from fuel point to fuel point, the foursome wind up at an isolated suburban mall that still has electricity. No one is around but the zombies. The survivors speculate that the zombies have some residual memories of being at the mall and it being a happy place. Realizing that the mall has everything they could ever need, they decide to clear out the zombies, secure the entrances, and set up a good life for themselves. Naturally plans don't go according to plan (otherwise, where would the drama be?) and their heavenly haven slowly becomes overrun with problems.<br /><br />The film's best part is the mall sequence, though it takes a good 40 minutes or so to get there. After they've cleaned up the place, they try to live the good life like there's nothing going on outside. Eventually television broadcasts stop so no information is available, furthering their fantasy of isolated security. They try to live happily with all the jewelry, clothes, and electronics they could ever want. The emptiness of consumerism eventually hits them but too late, because new zombies and a biker gang are on their way. Romero is merciless in mocking the low ambitions of all the characters. The drama and the satire are well blended if over the top at times. During the final battle with the zombies, an extended sequence goes comic with, among other gags, the humans throwing pies in the faces of the zombies. I know it sounds like I'm making it up, but I swear I am not! A good five minutes of slap-stick precede the more horrific situation when the zombies get the upper hand and begin to take back the mall and pick apart the living humans (yes, I do mean that literally).<br /><br />The gore is very extreme. I'm not really a fan of gore though I can take it if the story is interesting enough or the characters engage me. So I did make it to the end of this film, but just barely. The social satire is surprisingly fresh though Romero is a little too pessimistic for my tastes.<br /><br />Another thing to note--several different versions of this film exist. The American theatrical release is different from the international release (Italian horror master Dario Argento made that cut of the film, apparently with less humor and more music) which is different from the \\"extended\\" edition which has another 12 minutes that was reinserted by someone other than Romero and Argento. I'm not sure which cut I saw though judging by the humor and lack of music it was the American cut. I watched on BBC's iPlayer, so maybe it is the Argento cut. I don't think I'll watch it again to find out.

This movie tells the Formula One career of a driver in a way only Ayrton Senna could be attempted to be encapsulated: Through those that were there.<br /><br />Coming up through the ranks of a gokart racer, Senna made it quite clear that he set his sights for Formula One. He broke into a brotherhood that seemed to be weary of outsiders (those from outside of Europe). Senna called ranking officals out in public on their handlings of situations. One can only hope that Senna made Formula One a better sport because of his presence.<br /><br />He was a purist driver. He believed in racing like this because of his gokart background which he stated was about as racing as it could get. Gokarting had no politics, no electronics, nothing but pure racing. He pulled that approach into Formula One and stood out immediately. He showed the world how to race the most advanced cars in the rain. Because of his purist approach, he rubbed many people the wrong way.<br /><br />Sometimes Senna was conflicted. His legendary battles with one-time teammate and rival Alain Prost exposed a personality that perhaps Senna would regret. He dabbled in the affairs of European racers and struggled with impure approach to racing. His call for purity included the safety for drivers. He was the voice of drivers who seemingly were afraid to speak up to the directors of FIA. Senna was more than happy to express his displeasure.<br /><br />It is interesting to note that as Formula One was ushering in an electronic age, Senna would lose his life in a car provided by a team that began the new age. It was in the Williams at Imola (a weekend that in retrospect could have been warning the world) on lap six that Senna left the world. One can only feel a sense of loss watching the weekend at Imola as well as the on-board camera as we, the viewer, ride along with Ayrton on that fateful lap with Schumacher following on his wing.<br /><br />He raced at the end of an inspirational era. Race cars were no longer ground-breaking. They were only being corralled. Instead of innovation, cars were only being limited. As stated before, hopefully Senna's existance in FIA brought about some change. Formula One has not (to date) had a fatality since. One can only hope that it was Senna's sacrifice that prevented many other deaths in the spirit of motor sports. Even in death Senna earned very little respect from European directors in Formula One.

Cap and Cyclops' warring agendas are clearly laid out. I like that I couldn't predict which side Wolverine would choose. I like Spider-Man's desperate last stand in issue #9, a fantastic sequence reminiscent in some ways of his kickass guest appearance way back in UNCANNY X-MEN #190-191. I like that there are long-term ramifications. I like that Captain America admits that he could've done more to support the X-Men (and mutants in general) in their times of need. AvX sets the stage for DC's New Fifty-Tw... er, Marvel NOW! And while I'm holding out on judgment, I will say that the debut issues of UNCANNY AVENGERS and ALL-NEW X-MEN are very promising.<br /><br />I was entertained, yeah, but there are issues. It's a mammoth volume, packed with one skirmish after another. And maybe that's the downfall. AVENGERS VS. X-MEN feels padded. The sheer number of battles eventually has a numbing effect. The plot twist with the Phoenix Five adds a shady element in that I simply cannot buy that a person imbued with the Phoenix force would have so much trouble taking out even the Avengers' heavy hitters. Even the Hulks should've been no match. Again, it sucks that the main series only gives you the broad strokes. There are so many side plots going on that you're forced to check out various other titles to really keep track. What does it say when my favorite bit about this whole thing turns out to be a faux, tongue-in-cheek segment in issue #6 of AVX: VERSUS in which Cyclops and Captain America trade in mutual verbal abuse:<br /><br />- Cyclops: \\"You're the most boring superhero on the planet Earth. And that includes Green Arrow.\\"<br />- Captain America: \\"I'M boring? I thought your mutant power was vanilla.\\"<br /><br />Also in this issue #6 of AVX: VERSUS: Squirrel Girl vs. Pixie and Toad vs. Jarvis.<br /><br />Oliver Copiel, Adam Kubert, and John Romita, Jr. handle the art chores. Mostly, their art was on point, but, sometimes, shaky.<br /><br />AVENGERS VS. X-MEN collects - in the order presented in the volume - POINT ONE #1, AVENGERS VS. Navy SEAL team 3 is a very experienced team that had been to Afghanistan on multiple occasions. This is the main reason why Chris wanted to join this esteemed team. I still ache for my failure to protect them.\\"Pg. 397. Chris is like this because he doesn't see himself as an amazing person. This book describes the life of the great American soldier Chris Kyle and his life in the Navy Seals.<br /> This is an important book to anyone who wants to learn about a man's journey and how he overcomes challenges and obstacles on a daily basis. There are times and situations of hardships but in everyone's life one shouldn't give up no matter what. Chris puts a lot of emphases on his family and country showing readers these are very important and should be treasured. One of my favorite things about this book is his honesty to the American people.  There is a lot about war that most people don't want to talk about but Chris wanted people to understand on a level that most people didn't understand. He tells the reader about both his family problems with his wife and kids and his wartime problems throughout his military career. In conclusion, Chris Kyle is a true American hero who communicates to the reader that war is a stressful and challenging issue for a family and for an individual person to handle. This book is amazing book and should receive 5 out of 5 stars in any review. Another great book that shows the troubles and life of a Navy SEAL is Lone Survivor. This is a must read for anyone who loves action with a touch of family values and a man's love for God and country.

Matty wants to keep their hookups under the radar, while Jenna, who goes along for the sake of closeness, is internally screaming for more. How can one teenage brain handle all this craziness at once?<br /><br />When this show started, a little part of me was going, \\"Alright, c'mon. You've been out of high school for nearly three years, do you really need to be watching some show about a bunch of teeangers having sex and creating drama? Her main reason for her attitude is, rather than power or popular right or what have you, that she is angry for having been cursed with a \\"fat gene.\\" This is another reason I find her likeable. Whether at the bottom or top of the high school food chain, every single girl has, at some point in her life, had body issues, and it's interesting to see the villain of the show have such a weakness.<br /><br />A nice little bonus is that when the episodes initially aired on tv, little \\"after show\\" bits would go up on the MTV site. B007V65R54|5.0|Statisticians rarely become superstars, but Nate Silver is getting close. This is the guy who writes the FiveThirtyEight.com blog for the New York Times and has correctly predicted the outcome of the last two presidential elections in virtually every one of the 50 states. But Silver is no political maven weaned on election trivia at his parents' dinner table: he earned his stripes as a prognosticator supporting himself on Internet poker and going Billy Beane of the Oakland A's (Moneyball) one better by developing an even more sophisticated statistical analysis of what it takes to win major league baseball games. And, by the way: Silver is just 34 years old as I write this post.<br /><br />The Signal and the Noise is Silver's first book, and what a book it is! As you might expect from this gifted enfant terrible, the book is as ambitious as it is digestible. Written in an easy, conversational style, The Signal and the Noise explores the ins and outs of predicting outcomes not just in politics, poker, and sports (baseball and basketball) as well as the stock market, the economy, and the 2008 financial meltdown, weather forecasting, earthquakes, epidemic disease, chess, climate change, and terrorism.<br /><br />Fundamentally, The Signal and the Noise is about the information glut we're all drowning in now and how an educated person can make a little more sense out of it. As Silver notes, \\"The instinctual shortcut we take when we have `too much information' is to engage with it selectively, picking out the parts we like and ignoring the remainder, making allies with those who have made the same choices and enemies of the rest.\\" What else could explain why Mitt Romney was \\"shell-shocked\\" and Karl Rove was astonished by Romney's loss in a presidential election that every dispassionate observer knew was going Obama's way?<br /><br />Silver asserts that \\"our predictions may be more prone to failure in the era of Big Data. As there is an exponential increase in the amount of available information, there is likewise an exponential increase in the number of hypotheses to investigate . . . But the number of meaningful relationships in the data . . . is orders of magnitude smaller. Nor is it likely to be increasing at nearly so fast a rate as the information itself; there isn't any more truth in the world than there was before the Internet or the printing press. Most of the data is just noise, as most of the universe is filled with empty space.\\"<br /><br />Sadly, it's not just in politics that bias clouds judgment and leads to erroneous conclusions. \\"In 2005, an Athens-raised medical researcher named John P. Ioannidis published a controversial paper titled `Why Most Published Research Findings Are False.' The paper studied positive findings documented in peer-reviewed journals: descriptions of successful predictions of medical hypotheses carried out in laboratory experiments. It concluded that most of these findings were likely to fail when applied in the real world. Bayer Laboratories recently confirmed Ioannidis's hypothesis. They could not replicate about two-thirds of the positive findings claimed in medical journals when they attempted the experiments themselves.\\"<br /><br />In general, Silver's thesis runs, \\"We need to stop, and admit it: we have a prediction problem. We love to predict things -- and we aren't very good at it. . . We focus on those signals that tell a story about the world as we would like it to be, not how it really is. We ignore the risks that are hardest to measure, even when they pose the greatest threats to our well-being. We make approximations and assumptions about the world that are much cruder than we realize. We abhor uncertainty, even when it is an irreducible part of the problem we are trying to solve.\\"<br /><br />There's more: Silver relates the work of a UC Berkeley psychology and political science professor named Philip Tetlock, who categorizes experts as either foxes or hedgehogs (in deference to an ancient Greek poet who wrote, \\"The fox knows many little things, but the hedgehog knows one big thing.\\"). Hedgehogs traffic in Big Ideas and often hew to ideologies; these are the people who talk to the press and are frequently found on TV talk shows. Foxes are cautious types who carefully examine and weigh details before reaching conclusions. Not surprisingly, Tetlock found that \\"The more interviews that an expert had done with the press . . . the worse his predictions tended to be.\\"<br /><br />In other words, Be afraid. Be very afraid. If the people who supposedly know what they're talking about often really don't, how can the rest of us figure out what's going on?ncient Greek poet who wrote, \\"The fox knows many little things, but the hedgehog knows one big thing.\\"). Foxes are cautious types who carefully examine and weigh details before reaching conclusions. Be very afraid. If the people who supposedly know what they're talking about often really don't, how can the rest of us figure out what's going on?

B003WE9TU8|5.0|The writing in this book is very mature and fast-paced. The content flows seamlessly from one page to the next.
1401224792|4.0|This is a book for people who have been reading Fables. It makes allusions and references to the ongoing comic series and ends in such a way as to allow the new locations and characters to be revisited in the ongoing. If you have no familiarity with the setting I would recommend a different starting point, such as [[ASIN:140122427X Fables: The Deluxe Edition Book One]] or [[ASIN:1401203698 Fables: 1001 Nights of Snowfall]]. That said, you will still be able to follow the plot and enjoy the story if you haven't read Fables before, but won't get everything out of it that is offered.<br /><br />I have been reading, and loving, the series for all of its more than 120 issues. I've followed the exploits of spin-off series, mini-series, and novels. I'm seriously considering attending a get-together in Rochester, MN, in Spring 2013 being put on by the author and several of his friends who write and illustrate similar comics.<br /><br />I really liked this story. I will avoid spoilers in my review. Bigby Wolf, the big, bad wolf of story tales, is the protagonist. He is an immortal demi-god, son of the North Wind. The book opens with Bigby exploring the American Midwest. The author uses the trope of Bigby writing a journal/status report to back home in order to give the reader background on the Fables setting. This trope is used throughout the book to fill in narrative and give us insight into Bigby's thoughts. We flash back to him killing Nazis behind the front-lines of WWII (also seen in issues #28 and #29 of the original series, almost 100 issues and eight years ago). And then the rest of the story deals with him in the present day as he is surprised to discover in Iowa a legacy of that Nazi-slaying he thought he had left in Germany. After the somewhat slow prelude the story really takes off. I took a couple nights to get through the first couple chapters. But then I finished the rest all in one sitting, not wanting to put it down. I look forward to the introduced characters and the town of Story City popping up in and the results of this encounter affecting the main continuity of the Fables series.<br /><br />The writing is pretty good. If you read Fables you'll get what you expect from the author. If anything he compresses the narrative more than usual by use of the journal writing trope and more abrupt scene and time changes. I appreciated this. I think he has a tendency to go on too much in the main series. I wouldn't have wanted this story to be any longer. The two main antagonists go through a change in characterization between time periods that I don't think was well handled. I don't want to spoil anything. But I'll say they act one way in the beginning of the book, but when we encounter them later they are not nearly as well defined. And I don't think the way Bigby reacts to them later makes sense or is well-explained given his attitude towards them earlier.<br /><br />In the ongoing series, this story would have taken seven or eight issues to tell, at least. And it would have had to be significantly altered in its depiction to be printed even in Vertigo, the HBO of comics. I like that I was able to get the entire story all at once without waiting for its serialization. And while I really enjoyed this story, I'm glad it didn't push out the current main narrative of the series. I hope the author continues to do this. I wouldn't mind an annual or even bi-annual hard-cover original graphic novel that explores the (almost too many) characters and settings of the Fables universe.<br /><br />The illustrations are mostly good if inconsistent. A spare style and flat coloring is used that I like. I'd almost call it old-school but absolutely call it refreshing compared to the hyper-detailed tracing and over-saturated and gradient-ed stuff that fills most super-hero comics on the comic-store shelves. It is very different from the style used by Mark Buckingham, Steve Leialoha, or Shawn McManus in the main series and as such it doesn't \\"feel\\" as \\"Fables-y\\" until you get into the story. There are splash illustrations are very good to excellent (and exceptionally graphic). Unfortunately, one artist does not handle penciling and inking of the entire book. Instead four different people have credits which really hurts the consistency of the illustrations. That combined with the generally flat coloring and very similar Aryan-like appearance of most of the secondary characters hurts their characterization and makes the book confusing to read at times. I find it baffling that more than one person was required to illustrate this book. There's really no excuse for it: this is a book, not a monthly series with its tight page-a-day deadlines. Not taking the time it needed really hurts the final product.<br /><br />The physical artifact is excellent. It is a substantial-feeling hard cover with nice dust jacket. The cover fully reproduces the dust jacket art. The binding is glued but is very tight. The paper is thick and semi-glossy. The ink is dark, sticks to the page and not your fingers, and looks good. You'll enjoy holding, reading, and displaying this book and it stands up well to its cover price.<br /><br />This is a book for adults, or near adults. There are extremely graphic depictions of nudity, violence, and sex. Genetic purity, human experimentation, the creation of an incestuous \\"master race\\", murder, suicide, and infanticide are depicted or implied. As depicted, this couldn't be filmed and shown on even premium cable channels like HBO without major editing or censoring. A movie version would be rated at least NC-17. And it would't be printed as-is in the monthly Vertigo-(the HBO of comics)-published series. I'm pretty liberal but wouldn't let my children see this book until they were in late high school.<br /><br />In conclusion, this is a good if not great addition to the Fables canon. I enjoyed it, and recommend it.<br /><br />(A note on my star rating. I use a four-star review method: one star means I hated it, two means I didn't like it, four means I liked it, and five means I loved it. What does three mean? Exactly.)

0307341577|5.0|I picked \\"Dark Places\\" up on a whim: \\"Gone Girl\\" keeps popping up in my recommendations, but it doesn't seem like something I'd enjoy.  I figured I'd give one of Flynn's other novels a try first that sounded more up my alley to test out her waters.  Wow.  Just... wow.  I blew through this book in two nights.  Not because it was quick-reading or fluff, but because I could not stop reading: I had to force myself to put it down and go to bed.<br /><br />For people complaining that the protagonist is unsympathetic and the the story too dark and violent... I don't know what you were expecting from a book about a woman who at seven years old witnesses her entire family being violently murdered.  Of course she's hardened, of course she's damaged, and of course the description of the killings are going to drill their way into your psyche and not let go: to me that's the sign of a brilliant writer.  Anyone who can make me need to step back for a moment and process the emotions and horror of the characters of a book, yet at the same time make me absolutely NEED to grab it and learn what's going to happen next, knowing full well it isn't going to be good, has a very rare and astounding talent.<br /><br />The parallel progression of Libby's life at both age seven and as an adult are brilliantly intertwined.  You know what is going to happen to her family, there is ZERO doubt: it's established in the first few paragraphs.  Yet you still hold on to that tiny hope that something will change- again, another sign of amazing writing.<br /><br />There's nothing pretty about it, and although it is an extremely satisfying conclusion, the ending is just as shocking, emotionally brutal, and harried as the rest of the novel.<br /><br />Do not pass this one up.  Make some coffee, get cozy, and prepare to hunker down with a meaty, intelligent, page-turner that you will want to start over as soon as you are done.

0307474275|1.0|Many novels are flawed, saddled with implausible plots, boring characters, and poor writing.  In the normal course of things, such books suffer the fate of Natural Selection: given small production runs, little-to-no publicity, and ignored by readers, they soon find their way to the sale tables, to be remaindered at bargain-basement prices, or to be recycled as packing material for more notable efforts.<br /><br />Unfortunately, some novels are not merely mediocre; they are painfully, even excruciatingly, bad.  Like George W. Bush and instant mashed potatos, their very existence calls out for attention, some effort to expose their many deficiencies to the harsh light of day, and to inform the public that they have the right to (and ought to demand) better.  Dan Brown's The Da Vinci Code is one such travesty recently perpetrated on the reading public.  It is living proof that whatever is popular is not necessarily good, and readers who doubt this distinction should invest in a good dictionary.<br /><br />To be fair, the plot of The Da Vinci Code is clever and well-conceived: it is an action thriller aimed at the movie-going public.  The frenetic pace has our intrepid hero, Harvard Professor of &#34;Symbology&#34; Dr. Robert Langdon, accompanied by French police code-breaker (and descendent of Jesus) Sophie Neveu, dashing from Paris to it's outskirts, on to London, then Scotland, and back again to Paris, in search of the Holy Grail, no less -- all in the space of less than twenty-four-hours!  It is, in short a skillful blend of the pace of Mission Impossible with the religious-historical storyline of The Name of the Rose.  Think Umberto Eco Lite.<br /><br />Moreover, the novel is badly overwritten, right from it's opening line: &#34;Renowned curator Jacques Sauni&egrave;re staggered through the vaulted archway...&#34;  Could his fame possibly be postponed until we know a little more about his predicament?  Brown seems addicted to the liberal use of exclamation marks and italicised phrases labouring the obvious, like a ham-actor villain in a silent movie looking directly at the audience and leering in a menacing manner: &#34;This is my fault!&#34;  &#34;Is she out of her mind?&#34;  &#34;If I hadn't seen it with my own eyes&#34;  &#34;I'll just take a fast peek!&#34;  &#34;There must be something here!&#34;  &#34;Could that be it?&#34;  &#34;I'm dreaming.  A dream.  What else could this be?&#34;  And so on.<br /><br />Brown's plot devices range from the merely silly to the outrageous.  In supporting his claims about hidden messages in works of art he ludicrously weaves in a reference to Walt Disney's The Lion King.  More disturbingly, Brown plays fast and loose with history.  A sample: in his preface, Brown claims &#34;all descriptions of artwork...in this novel are accurate.&#34;  Yet in the service of his plot Brown shamelessly reverses the figures of Jesus and St. John the Baptist in Leonardo's painting, Madonna of the Rocks, even though it's obvious who is whom (St. John has his signature staff, and Jesus is blessing him - not the other way around.)  Nor is the archangel Uriel making a slitting-his-throat gesture (as asserted), but is simply pointing at John.  Finally, Brown bases his entire plot on the existence of a secret society called The Priory of Sion, allegedly founded in 1099, whose existence is confirmed by medieval documents deposited in the Biblioth&egrave;que Nationale in Paris.  Described as &#34;fact&#34; at the beginning of the book, the Priory of Sion never actually existed.  Far from having a history that spanned more than a millennium, the Priory was a hoax perpetrated by Pierre Plantard, a con-victed con-man who in 1956 planted the bogus &#34;medieval&#34; documents in the National Library's files.  I have no problem at all with novelists inventing &#34;facts&#34; to lend their stories credence; after all, that's what fiction is.  But it's downright misleading -- and sucks in the uninformed -- to describesuch claims as facts before the novel itself begins.  Brown shamelessly presents fiction as fact in the service of his plot -- and that's what makes The Da Vinci Code so pernicious.<br /><br />To say that The Da Vinci Code is overrated is to abuse the term.  Its outstanding virtue is that it lures readers into bookstores; in the process they may stumble across other, more worthwhile, reads.  Its lesser merit is that its very size (weighing in at nearly 500 pages) makes it easy to throw it away.Brown shamelessly presents fiction as fact in the service of his plot -- and that's what makes The Da Vinci Code so pernicious.  To say that The Da Vinci Code is overrated is to abuse the term.  Its outstanding virtue is that it lures readers into bookstores; in the process they may stumble across other, more worthwhile, reads.  Its lesser merit is that its very size (weighing in at nearly 500 pages) makes it easy to throw it away.

B007ZBWJXI|2.0|Asia's latest is another compressed pop studio piece of pap. There is no prog so let's get that out of the way before we go further. NO PROG. So we have John Wetton, great as ever on vocals and bass, Geoff Downes marvellous on keyboards, Steve Howe eternally brilliant on guitars, and Carl Palmer a living legend on drums. They play nicely and won't trouble any metronome from maintaining a steady 4/4 beat. The metronome swings back and forth happily with this knowledge after years of battering from the complexities of ELP, King Crimson, and Yes.<br /><br />The album is called 'XXX' in commemoration of the thirtieth anniversary of the album release, but don't dare Google Asia XXX while at work. Roger Dean's artwork is sublime paying homage to the debut album which is a nice touch, the proggiest thing about it as usual. It signifies The Chinese Year of The Water Dragon, 2012.<br /><br />The album boasts some extremely popular radio hits already, I am talking about 'Bury Me In Willow', infectious chorus, steady 4/4 tempo, has a nice melody throughout and sweet vocals. It almost sounds like 80s AOR. It is nice. It did jam into my skull and I was walking around humming the tune; altogether now 'give me no standard, no eulogy, No red, white and blue, no sceptre and no cloak, Just bury me in willow, not in oooooooooooooooaaaaaaaaaak.'.<br /><br />'Tomorrow The World' is also popular. Great melody that wraps itself around your cerebral chord and refuses to let you sleep. The catchy chorus is maddeningly viral on your senses, you won't forget it if you listen too much. It is also nice. The synths sound like Europe's 'The Final Countdown' but it's a retro synth sound I agree with. The ending lead break is cool but not long enough, Mr Howe.<br /><br />'No Religion' is even catchier than any of the other songs especially the maddening chorus 'Got no religion, just living hell, If I had some money, I'd drop it all in the wishing well, Stand in the doorway, wait for the man to come, Got no religion, and my life's just begun.' It has a decent keyboard solo and solid structure. Repetitive, sure, but that's radio friendly for you.<br /><br />'Face on the Bridge' is the first single from 'XXX', reaching number 1. This and the first three songs are longer than 6 minutes but still are not as creative as one might desire given the talented skill on hand. Seeing the length of the songs made me wonder would they give us some virtuoso soloing? The answer is a resounding no. I could envisage housewives jumping about humming this while they make the dinner. Even my wife liked me listening to this pap, and she can't stand all that complex prog.<br /><br />'Judas' annoys me lyrically and repetitively, but the worst song is 'Reno', a bonus track best left on the cutting room floor. It is dreadful slush. 'Ghost of a Chance' is also a dreary meandering syrupy ballad. Nice to put the old iphone in the air and sway during a concert, but give me Rush's 'Closer to the Heart' any day. The other bonus track is okay, 'I Know How You Feel', another saccharine lovey dovey ballad, but this has a strong melody that really is endearing.<br /><br />At the end of listening to this AOR album one is left with a warm feeling, strong melodies and a mainstream radio sound does not hurt at all and at least Asia can create music that appeals to a mass audience. They are not particularly a band I would recommend or listen to often, but having stated that, this is still an album with quality production and packaging. All the talent of the prog legends though is buried in girly papsmear pop pith and it is a mediocre album with about 3 great songs. This album epitomises all the reasons I can't abide listening to top 40 radio hits; it is so syrupy the treacle pours out of my speakers. If this were an AOR site I would give it 3 stars, but it is not as good as their debut and I could only give that 2 stars so 2 stars it is again. I'm off to clean my ears out with some decent prog now, where's that King Crimson CD?here's that King Crimson CD?
